# We can make the code even shorter with arrow functions

So far in this book, we’ve focused on two different ways to make functions: _function declarations_ and _function expressions_. There’s a third way to make functions, and that’s the _arrow function_. Arrow functions are almost the same thing as anonymous function expressions (except for a couple of important differences we’ll get to shortly), and they can make your code even more concise.

Let’s take a look at how you make an arrow function. We’ve already stepped you through how to convert a function declaration into an anonymous function expression like this:

###### 

We can convert the handler function into an arrow function like this:

**Step 1** : Get rid of the function keyword:
    
    
    **window.onload =~~function~~() { alert("Yeah, that page loaded!"); };**

**Step 2** : Add the arrow:

###### 

**Step 3** : Get rid of the { and } (you can do this only if there’s just one statement in the function):
    
    
    **window.onload = () = > { alert("Yeah, that page loaded!"); +;**

**Step 4** : Finally, get rid of the ; (again, you can do this only if there’s just one statement in the function):
    
    
    **window.onload = () = > alert("Yeah, that page loaded!");;**

###### Note

Make sure you remove only the semicolon that is on the alert statement, not the one on the assignment.

The final result is:
    
    
    **window.onload = () = > alert("Yeah, that page loaded!");**

###### Note

Shorter!

###### 

**The arrow function syntax might look a little odd at first.** You’re right, the function keyword is gone completely in arrow functions. You can think of the arrow:

**= >**

in:
    
    
    **() = > alert("Yeah, that page loaded!")**

as taking the place of the function keyword. The browser sees that arrow and knows that the parentheses on the left of the arrow and the code on the right of the arrow define a function—in this case, an arrow function.

As we said earlier, an arrow function is almost the same thing as an anonymous function expression; it’s just written more concisely.

# Creating arrow functions

Arrow functions are particularly useful when you’ve got short, one-line, anonymous functions with no or just a few parameters, and you only need those functions once. A window `onload` or `setTimeout` handler can be a great place to use an arrow function. Typically, these are short, one-use functions, so there’s no need to give them a name, and you want to write them as concisely as you can.

Let’s make an arrow function out of a simple function that takes a number, adds one to it, and returns that value. We’ll start by writing that function as an anonymous function expression, like this:
    
    
    **let addOne = function(n) { return n + 1; };**

Now, using the rules we already talked about, we can convert this to an arrow function by removing the `function` keyword, { and }, and semicolon after the `return` and adding an arrow, like this:

###### 

However, if you try this code, you’ll get an error. Why?

There’s one more “rule of conciseness” you need to apply in this arrow function: when the only statement in an arrow function is a return statement, you must eliminate the `return`. The value of the expression that’s left is automatically returned by the function (instead of undefined). If we apply that rule, we get:
    
    
    **let addOne = (n) = > return n + 1;**

If you really want to keep the `return`, or you have a function with multiple statements in the body of the function (or both), you must use the { and } around the function body. For example, let’s rewrite `addOne` as an arrow function with multiple statements, including a return statement:

###### 

Lastly, before we look at how we might use arrow functions in our code, how about a function with two parameters? We can do that like this:
    
    
    **let addTwoNums = (n, m) = > n + m;**

###### Note

Just like in a regular function, we put multiple parameters in ( ), separated by a comma.

# there are no Dumb Questions

**Q:** **Using these anonymous functions like this seems really esoteric. Do I really need to know this stuff?**

**A:** You do. Anonymous function expressions are used frequently in JavaScript code, so if you want to be able to read other people’s code, or understand JavaScript libraries, you’re going to need to know how these work, and how to recognize them when they’re being used.

**Q:** **Is using an anonymous function expression really better? I think it just complicates the code and makes the code hard to follow and read.**

**A:** Give it some time. With practice, you’ll be able to parse code like this more easily when you see it, and there really are lots of cases where this syntax decreases code complexity, makes the code’s intention clearer, and cleans up your code. That said, overuse of this technique can definitely lead to code that is quite hard to understand—but stick with it, and it’ll get easier to read and more useful as you get the hang of it. You’re going to encounter lots of code that makes heavy use of anonymous functions, so it’s a good idea to incorporate this technique into your code toolbelt.

**Q:** **If first class functions are so useful, how come other languages don’t have them?**

**A:** Ah, but they do (and even the ones that don’t are considering adding them). For instance, languages like Scheme and Scala have fully first class functions like JavaScript does. Other languages, like PHP, Java (in the newest versions), C#, and Objective C have some or most of the first class features that JavaScript does. As more people are recognizing the value of having first class functions in a programming language, more languages are supporting them. Each language does it a little differently, however, so be prepared for a variety of approaches as you explore this topic in other languages.

**Q:** **The arrow in the arrow function looks a lot like the “greater than or equals” operator; can it also mean that sometimes, in the right context?**

**A:** No. >= is a always a conditional operator and => is always indicating an arrow function. If you try to write 3 => 4, you’ll get an error. Try it in the console!

**Q:** **You said an arrow function is slightly different from a function expression—how?**

**A:** Arrow functions are intended to be a more concise alternative to function expressions, as we’ve seen. The main goal is conciseness. However, arrow functions have slightly different semantics from function expressions. For instance, they shouldn’t be used as methods, because this is not assigned the value of the object whose method you’re calling (like it is in regular object methods). There are some other subtle differences, but that’s the main one.

**Q:** **How do I know if I should use a function expression or an arrow function?**

**A:** If you’re passing a one-off function to another function, that’s a great place to use an arrow function. If, however, you’re writing a method of an object, or a handler for an event, like onload or onclick, use a function expression instead, because in those cases, you’ll probably need this to be assigned a reference to the object whose method you’re calling, or the element object associated with the event handler.

We’ll see quite a few places later in the chapter where using an arrow function is concise and a great solution, so there’ll be lots more examples coming up.

###### 

**Anonymous functions, and their arrow function shorthand, open up all kinds of new possibilities.** We’ve already seen one example of this, when we used functions with events in the last chapter. If you think through the functions we supplied to our events, they can all be specified as anonymous functions. Anonymous and first-class functions are also going to allow us to parameterize other functions with computation in something called _higher-order_ functions. We’ll get to that shortly and see its power. We’ll even go further in the next chapter and explore how these functions enable us to use a powerful technique called _closures_ to enable keeping private state in our functions. And ahead in your studies, you’ll even discover functions as the foundation of how JavaScript handles asynchronous computation.

That’s a lot! We’d better get started.

**Sam** : I got a call from Webville Cola, and they need help with their product data. They want to be able to sort their products by any property, like name, bottles sold, soda color, calories per bottle, and so on, but they want it flexible in case they add more properties in the future.

**Mara** : Oh, I’ve got a simpler way than that, but it requires you to use your knowledge of first class functions.

**Sam** : I like hearing simple! But how do first class functions fit in? That sounds complicated to me.

**Mara** : Not at all. It’s as easy as writing a function that knows how to compare two values, and then passing that to another function that does the real sorting for you.

**Alex** : How does the function we’re writing work, exactly?

**Mara** : Well, instead of handling the entire sort, all you need to do is write a function that knows how to compare two values. Say you want to sort by a product property like the number of bottles sold. You set up a function like this:

    **// code to compare here**
    **}**

###### Note

This function needs to take two products and then compare them.

We can fill in the details of the code in a minute, but for now, the key is that once you have this function, you just pass it to a sort function and that sort function does the work for you—it just needs you to help it know how to compare things.

And, when `sort` is done, the `products` array will be sorted by whatever criteria `compareSold` used to sort the values.

**Alex** : So if I’m sorting how many bottles are sold, those are numbers, so the `compareSold` function just needs to determine which value is less or greater?

**Mara** : Right. We’d better take a closer look at how the array sort works...

# Understanding the array sort method

JavaScript arrays provide a `sort` method that, given a function that knows how to compare two items in the array, sorts the array for you. There’s a lot in that sentence, so let’s break it down with a simple example. Here’s a simple array of numbers:
    
    
    **let numbersArray = [60, 50, 62, 58, 54, 54];**

###### Note

Just an ordinary array filled with some numbers that we’d like to sort.

Now, there are all kinds of ways we could sort these numbers, but let’s say we want to sort them in descending order from large to small. If we use the array’s `sort` method with no argument at all, that’s not what we get:

###### 

The array’s `sort` method can also take a single argument, a comparison function. Here’s how that works: the comparison function takes two items, and compares them. It returns –1 if the first item is less than the second, 0 if they are equal, or 1 if the second item is greater than the first. Let’s see if we can write a comparison function to compare numbers:

###### 

# Putting it all together

Now that we’ve written a `compare` function, all we need to do is call the `sort` method on `numbersArray` and pass it the function. Here’s how we do that:

###### 

# Sharpen your pencil

###### 

You know that the comparison function we pass to sort needs to return a number greater than 0, equal to 0, or less than 0, depending on the two items we’re comparing. To get a descending-ordered sort, here’s what we do: if the first item is less than the second, we return a value greater than 0; if the first item is equal to the second, we return 0; and if the first item is greater than the second, we return a value less than 0.

    **];**

###### Note

So what are we going to sort first? Let’s start with sorting by the number of bottles sold, in ascending order. To do this, we’ll need to compare the `sold` property of each object. Now, one thing you should take note of is that because this is an array of product objects, the compare function is going to be passed two objects, not two numbers:

###### 

And of course, to use the `compareSold` function to sort the `products` array, we simply call the `products` array’s `sort` method:
    
    
    **products.sort(compareSold);**

###### Note

Remember that the sort method can be used for any kind of array (numbers, strings, objects), and for any kind of sort (ascending or descending). By passing in a compare function, we get flexibility and code reuse!

###### Note

Note: we’re sorting ascending this time, rather than descending, so notice the difference with our previous compare function.

# Introducing higher-order functions

###### 

We already know that JavaScript functions are first class values; we can pass then around and do anything with them we’d do with any other JavaScript type, whether that is storing them in a variable, passing them to a function, or returning them from a function. In fact, we have a name for functions that take one or more functions as arguments, or return a function (or both): _higher-order functions_.

You might be asking, what is the point? Well, we’ll return to the topic of returning functions from functions, but when we’re talking about passing functions to functions, why would we want to do that? Isn’t the point of a function to compute something?

Think about it like this: we pass parameters to functions to _parameterize_ them. We’re used to thinking about parameterizing functions with different values. If we have a function that draws buttons, we might parameterize the size, the label of the button, and the color, so one function can create many kinds of buttons.

So what are we parameterizing by passing a function to another function? How about _computation_? For instance, as we just saw, say we have a function that sorts arrays, and we’d like to tell it _how_ it should compare the items in the array it is sorting. We can do that by passing a function to the array’s `sort` method that computes a comparison of an array’s items. That’s a higher-order function!

> **Higher-order functions allow you to parameterize computation, which can provide a high degree of flexibility and reusability in your code.**

There are a few other higher-order functions you’re going to see a lot in JavaScript. These too work well with arrays. Let’s check them out:

  * □ **map** : `map` does some computation on each item in the array, mapping each value to a return value. The result is a new array of those return values.

  * □ **filter** : `filter` tests each item with some computation (which returns true or false). If the result is true (the item passes the test), the item is filtered into a new array.

  * □ **reduce** : `reduce` reduces an array to one value, using some computation and an accumulator.

  * □ **forEach** : `forEach` is another way to iterate over an array and do some computation on each item in the array.




# Filtering with higher-order functions

###### 

Let’s put our knowledge to use and filter Webville Cola’s products with the array’s `filter` method. To use `filter`, we write a function that performs a test on one array item and determines if it passes that test. We pass that function to the array’s `filter` method, which applies the function to every item in the array. If an item passes the test, that item is included in a new array created by `filter`; otherwise, it is excluded.

Let’s say Webville Cola is interested to know how many of its products are actually colas, as opposed to fruit-flavored drinks, water, or root beer. First, we’ll write a function to see if the name of a given cola from the `products` array includes the string “Cola” (this is our test):
    
    
    **let isCola = function(cola) {**
    **return (cola.name.includes("Cola"));**
    **};**

###### Note

Our function takes a product and tests to see if the name includes “Cola”.

We can now use our `isCola` function to filter out all the non-cola products in the `products` array and give us a new array containing only the colas:
    
    
    **let colas = products.filter(isCola);**
    **console.log(colas);**

###### Note

We pass the isCola function to the filter method, which applies it to every drink in the products array.

## How to get cases sold with map

The first thing we can do is convert the number of drinks sold for each product to the number of cases sold, by dividing each `sold` value by 12. We could write a for loop to do this...but instead, let’s use the higher-order array method `map`, like this:

###### 

The `map` method takes a function that’s applied to each drink in the `products` array and returns a value that is that drink’s `sold` value divided by 12. `map` takes all the values returned and gathers them up into a new array of the number of cases sold for each product in the `products` array.

In other words, we’re mapping an array of products to an array of cases sold.

Now that we have the cases sold for all our products, how can we turn that into the total number of cases sold?

## Get the total cases sold with reduce

Again, we could write a for loop to iterate through the `cases` array and add up the number of cases sold, but let’s use the higher-order array method, `reduce`, instead.

Like `filter` and `map`, `reduce` takes a function that is applied to each item in an array. However, it’s slightly different from `filter` and `map` in that it also takes a running total that gets accumulated each time the function is applied to another item in the array. That’s because the whole point of `reduce` is to turn the array into one value. In our case, that one value should be the total number of cases.

# Who Does What

Match the terms on the left to the descriptions on the right! Check your answers before moving on.

**map** | **Does some computation on each item in the array and creates a new array with the resulting values.**  
---|---  
**filter** | **Tests each item in an array with a boolean test; if the test is true, the item is added to a new array that is returned.**  
**reduce** | **Collapses an array to one value, using some computation and an accumulator.**  
**forEach** | **Iterates over an array and does some computation on each item in the array.**  
  
**Solution in“Who Does What ? Solution”**

###### 

**You got us.** The `forEach` array method is one more higher-order function you should know about. It gives us a way to iterate over an array and supply a function that does something to each item in the array. So unlike with, say, a for loop, we don’t specify any actual iteration; we just supply the function and `forEach` takes care of the rest. Let’s give it a try.

    **{ name: "Water", calories: 0, color: "clear", sold: 62123 }**
    **];**
    
    **products.forEach(cola = >** 
    **console.log(`Name: ${cola.name},** 
    **Calories: ${cola.calories},** 
    **Color: ${cola.color},** 
    **Sold: ${cola.sold}`)**
    **);**

###### Note

# Bullet Points

  * There are three ways to define a function: with a **function declaration** , with a **function expression** , or with an **arrow function**.

  * A **function reference** is a value that refers to a function.

  * When the browser evaluates a function declaration, it creates a function as well as a variable with the same name as the function, and stores the function reference in the variable.

  * When the browser evaluates a function expression or arrow function, it creates a function, and it’s up to you what to do with the function reference.

  * A function without a name is known as an **anonymous function**.

  * We often use anonymous functions as event handlers or to pass as one-off functions to other functions, like setTimeout.

  * **First class** values can be assigned to variables, included in data structures, passed to functions, or returned from functions.

  * Functions are first class values.

  * The goal of arrow functions is conciseness and expressivity.

  * **Arrow functions** are very similar to function expressions, except they generally shouldn’t be used as object methods or event handlers because the value of “this” will not be set to what you expect (i.e., the object whose method you’re calling or whose event you’re handling).

  * A **higher-order function** is a function that takes a function and/or returns a function.

  * The array method **sort** is a higher-order function that takes a function that knows how to compare two values in an array.

  * The function you pass to the sort method should return one of these values: a number greater than 0, 0, or a number less than 0. The sort method uses these values to sort an array in ascending or descending order.

  * Other higher-order functions that are array methods include map, filter, reduce, and forEach.

  * The **map** method takes a function that is applied to every item in the array and returns a new value that’s added to a new array. In other words, it maps from one array of values to another.

  * The **filter** method takes a function that is applied to every item in the array and returns true or false. If true is returned, then the value from the original array is added to a new array. In other words, it filters all items from an array that pass a test into a new array.

  * The **reduce** method takes a function that is applied to every item in the array and returns one value that is the result of accumulating results from the computation on each item in the original array. In other words, it reduces an array of values to one value.

  * The **forEach** method takes a function that is applied to every item in the array. It is a way of iterating over an array and performing computation on each item in the array.

  * The map, filter, and reduce methods are designed so they can be **chained** together into one long statement if so desired (without the need for intermediate variables).

# Chapter 11. Modern Syntax, Lexical Scope, and Closures: **_Serious functions_**

###### 

**You’ve put functions** In this chapter, we take it further; we get hard-core. We’ll show you how to up your syntax game with advanced techniques for handling arguments, parameters, and assignments. We’ll then take another look at scope and some of the finer points of how JavaScript manages it. This journey through the subtleties of scope brings us to the heart of closures—a concept often shrouded in mystery but pivotal in mastering JavaScript. In the end, you’ll be more expressive with your JavaScript than you thought possible. through their paces, but there’s more to learn.

###### Note

And if you have heard of a closure but don’t quite know what it is, you’re in the right place!

# Getting serious about functional syntax

You might think at this point there is nothing else to know about basic function syntax, but there are actually a number of syntactic enhancements that have been added to JavaScript that can make your use of functions even more straightforward and expressive. Before we get down to talking about scope and closures, let’s make sure you’re up to speed on everything you can do when calling and receiving values from functions.

###### 

## Streamlining code with default parameters

Let’s say we have a function, `greeting`, that takes a message and a recipient, like this:
    
    
    **function greeting(message, recipient) {**
    **console.log(message, recipient);**
    **}**

Let’s call `greeting` like this:

###### 

What do you think we get if we leave off an argument, like this?

###### 

JavaScript gives us a way to deal with this by providing a default parameter value, so if the caller doesn’t pass along an argument, that default value is used instead of `undefined`. It works like this:

###### 

Let’s give it a try:

###### 

You can specify a default value for any, all, or none of the parameters. Here, we provide default values for both parameters in `greeting`:
    
    
    **function greeting(message="Howdy", recipient="Earthling") {**
    **console.log(message, recipient);**
    **}**

However, note that if you want to use the default values for parameters that are before the parameter you specify, you’ll have to pass an argument of `undefined` for those parameters:
    
    
    **greeting(undefined, "Sam");**

###### Note

We need to specify undefined for any parameter we’d like a default value for, unless it is the last parameter.

###### 

# BE the Browser

###### 

**Your job is to act like you’re the browser. You need to evaluate the code and produce its output. We’ve already started it for you.**
    
    
    **function pitch(noun="AI", verb="powered", obj="scooter") {**
    **console.log(noun, verb, obj);**
    **}**
    **pitch("Human", "designed", "bicycle");**
    **pitch("Monkey", undefined, "typewriter");**
    **pitch();** 
    **pitch("Peddle");**
    **pitch(undefined, "designed");**
    **pitch(undefined, undefined, "robot");**

**Solution in“BE the Browser Solution”**

###### 

**Yes. We can use _rest_ parameters**. JavaScript provides rest parameters as a way to specify an indefinite number of arguments as an array. This can be very helpful when you don’t know the number of arguments a caller is going to pass to a function. Rest parameters are denoted by three dots `...` followed by the name of the array you’d like to hold the arguments, like this:
    
    
    **function greeting(message, ...recipients) {**
    **recipients.forEach(r = > console.log(message, r));**
    **}**

You can choose whatever variable name you like for the rest parameter. Note that the rest parameter must be the last parameter.

# Spreading out your arguments

Okay, we’ve looked at the parameter side of things; now let’s take a look at a way to pass arguments to our functions using the spread operator, otherwise known as `...` (three dots). Here’s how it works.

###### Note

Yup, spread and rest are both operators, but we say rest when it’s used with a function parameter and spread when it’s used with an argument.

Say we have an array along with a function called `sum`:
    
    
    **let numbers = [1, 2, 3, 4];**
    
    **function sum(a, b, c, d) {**
    **console.log("The answer is", a + b + c + d);**
    **}**

We’d like to pass each value in the `numbers` array to a different parameter in the function `sum`. You might think we have to do this:
    
    
    **sum(numbers[0], numbers[1], numbers[2], numbers[3]);**

But with the spread operator we can expand the array in place, like this:

###### 

Unlike with rest parameters, we can use this as much as we want in a function call:

###### 

We can even mix spread arguments with regular arguments:

###### 

# there are no Dumb Questions

**Q:** **Is the spread operator just for arrays?**

**A:** No, you can use spread with arrays, strings, the properties in an object, or any collection of values that is iterable (like Set or Map; see the [Appendix A](app01.html#appendix_leftovers_the_top_ten_topics_le)). In the case of strings, the spread operator breaks a character string into its individual characters.

**Q:** **I’ve believe I’ve seen the spread operator used outside of function calls; is that possible?**

**A:** Yes, you can use it when creating arrays as well. Say you want to use an existing array as the basis for a new one. You can use spread like this:
    
    
    let iceCream = ["vanilla", "mint"];
    let moreIceCream = [...iceCream, "peach"];

###### Note

moreIceCream contains [“vanilla”, “mint”, “peach"].

**Q:** **You said that spread works with objects. How does that work?**

**A:** Very similar to what we just did with arrays. We can create a new object based on an existing one:
    
    
    let obj1 = { a: 1, b: 2 };
    let obj2 = { ...obj1, c: 3 };

###### Note

obj2 is now {a: 1, b: 2, c: 3}.

**Q:** **Why should I care about all these fancy ways of passing arguments and specifying parameters?**

**A:** On the surface they may seem “fancy,” but they help us write code that is more readable and that causes less bugs. Also, you’re going to be looking at a lot of code written by other software developers, and you are sure to see these techniques being used, so you want to be able to easily understand when reading someone else’s code.

# There’s something we haven’t told you about functions...

###### 

In the previous chapter, we looked at several different ways to define functions:

  * We can define a function with a function declaration, like this:
        
        **function quack(num) {** 
        **for (let i = 0; i < num; i++) {**
        **console.log("Quack!");**
        **}**
        **}**

  * We can define a function with a function expression and assign it to a variable, like this:
        
        **let fly = function(num) {** 
        **for (let i = 0; i < num; i++) {**
        **console.log("Flying!");**
        **}**
        **};**

  * We can define a function as an anonymous function expression, just where we need it, like this:
        
        **setTimeout(function() {**
         **alert("Time to take the cookies out of the oven");**
         **}, 600000);**

  * And we can define a function as an arrow function, like this:
        
        **colas = products.filter((cola) = > cola.name.includes("Cola"));**




What we haven’t told you yet is that one of these is handled a bit differently when the browser loads your page and begins to evaluate your code. One of these is a bit special, because the browser looks for it and defines these functions before it does anything else with your code. Which one? Turn the page and find out...

# Function declarations are “hoisted”

###### 

When the browser parses the code in your page, it makes _two passes over your code_.

Here’s how _hoisting_ works: on the first pass—before it evaluates any code—the browser looks for function declarations. When the browser finds one, it creates a function and assigns the resulting reference to a variable with the same name as the function.

Once the browser has completed the first pass through the code and defined every function created with a function declaration, it’s time for the second pass through the code. On this second pass, the browser evaluates the code, assigning values to variables, executing statements, and invoking functions.

To see how this works, let’s revisit our `fly` and `quack` functions and add a bit of code to use them. We’ll take two passes over the code, just like the browser does.

**Pass 1:**

###### 

# We’ve done the function declarations; now we do everything else

###### 

We’ve completed the first pass through the code, and it’s time for the second pass. Let’s see what happens.

**Pass 2:**

###### 

###### 

**Yes, that’s what we’re saying.** Function declarations are hoisted and defined before your code begins executing; function expressions aren’t defined until they are _evaluated_. In other words, function expressions aren’t included in hoisting.

Now, in our example, both `quack` and `fly` are essentially global variables, so you might have thought `fly` would be defined everywhere, like `quack`. And this is true to an extent; however, `fly` doesn’t exist until it is actually _evaluated_ on the second pass that JavaScript makes through your code.

So what does this all mean? For starters, it means that you can place top-level (i.e., global) function declarations anywhere in your code—at the top, at the bottom, in the middle—and invoke them wherever you like. However, function expressions are only callable after they have been evaluated.

To see this for yourself, try moving the **if** statement _above_ where you define `fly` and `quack`. Let’s see what happens:

###### 

###### 

**You are correct!** Arrow functions are also not hoisted; in this context they behave exactly like anonymous functions, so make sure you’ve evaluated an arrow function before you use it.

Try moving the assignment to `fly` _above_ the **if** statement. Now what happens?

Make sure you understand why before you go on.

# We need to talk about scope

Why all this focus on scope? Well, we’ve reached that point in our journey where truly understanding scope is going to have some _big payoffs_. Let’s start by exploring why functions don’t necessarily need to have a global scope.

# A lexical scope refresher

Most languages, including JavaScript, use _lexical scope_ to determine where variables are visible in code. As we’ve said, lexical scope is determined by looking at the structure of your code, rather than by runtime behavior. So, you can print out any JavaScript program and clearly identify the lexical scope of any given variable. With respect to functions, we can think of the lexical scope of variables like this: variables defined inside a function are accessible only within that function and any nested functions. Let’s see what that looks like:

###### 

> **Variables defined inside a function are accessible only within that function and any nested functions.**

# Another look at our outer/inner functions

The same lexical scoping rules apply to nested blocks and nested functions. Let’s revisit our outer and inner functions from the previous exercise to see how this works and why we encountered an error, remembering that to figure out the scope of a variable or function we look for the closest enclosing block where that variable or function is defined:

###### 

###### 

**Of course!** First, to be a competent programmer you need to be aware of where your variables and functions are visible, so you can write correct code, right?

Beyond that, we’re just about to get into some techniques for using scope that are going to change your entire outlook on what is possible with functions. One of those techniques is to protect or _encapsulate_ some of the internal details of your code and expose only what is necessary to the outside world. This is going to keep your code organized, and it can help prevent bugs in your code too.

Let’s look at an example.

###### Note

And, as you’ll see shortly, understanding lexical scope is critical to understandng closures, which we can use to maintain state beyond function calls, which is sorta like magic. Don’t worry about this for now...we’ll get to it soon!

# Two important JavaScript scope rules

Here’s a handy cheat sheet you can copy and pin up next to your computer while you’re learning about scope in JavaScript. There are two rules you’ll want to commit to memory. Here they are:

  * Within a function, if you want to determine where a variable is defined, search in its enclosing blocks (function or statement blocks), from the most nested to the least.

###### 

  * JavaScript functions are _always_ evaluated in the same scoping environment in which they were defined.

###### 




# there are no Dumb Questions

**Q:** **When you say that lexical scope determines where a variable is defined, what do you mean?**

**A:** This means that the program’s structure determines the scope of the variables in it. Given a variable definition, you simply need to look at the block (function or statement) where the variable is defined, and that’s its entire scope. If that block has nested blocks, the variable is visible to those nested blocks. Given a variable used in a function or block, look at the enclosing blocks to find its definition. If you can’t find a variable’s definition in an enclosing block, then it must be global or it’s undefined.

**Q:** **Just so I’m clear, a local scope is created by a function or a block, right?**

**A:** That’s right. The typical case is you have a function with its own code block. But statements like if and while often have blocks, and if you define variables within those blocks, they are local to those blocks.

**Q:** **Do parameters get included in a function’s local scope too?**

**A:** Yes. As we’ve said before, you can consider parameters to be local variables in your functions, so they are included in the function’s scope as well.

**Q:** **What’s a scoping environment? Is that the same as a scope?**

**A:** You can think of a scope as being associated with a specific variable—as in, “What’s the scope of a?” Think of a scoping environment as every variable and function that is defined in a given scope.

**Q:** **Why is lexical scoping a good thing? I would have thought having everything defined in the global scope, so I can access every variable anywhere I want, might be better.**

**A:** We can see how you might think that, but the advantage of lexical scope is that we can always look at the structure of the code to determine the scope that’s in place when a variable is defined and when it’s used, and figure out what its value should be from that.

If every variable and function is accessible in the global scope, then you might end up with name conflicts, which can be a problem as applications get larger and multiple people are working on the code.

As we saw, lexical scope also allows us to encapsulate computation and data to control where it is visible. This can help to prevent unintended modifications by other code, and it helps us to keep our code organized and easier to maintain.

> **Given a variable, you simply need to look at the block where the variable is defined, and that’s its entire scope.**
> 
> **JavaScript functions are always evaluated in the same scoping environment in which they were defined.**

# Solving the mystery

Queue the _X-Files_ theme song. How is it we’re making use of a variable that was defined in a function within our lexical scope, but that has already completed its execution? As we’ve always understood it, local variables are created when a function starts and destroyed when a function completes. So how can we still be using this variable? Don’t worry, the truth is out there.

###### 

And it’s closer than you think: the reason this works is because of lexical scoping and a feature of JavaScript called a _closure_. In JavaScript, whenever a function is returned from another function, it is wrapped up with the function’s lexical environment, meaning all the variables in its outer scope that it might need in the future. You can think of this as the function remembering its _environment_ , even if the outer function has long since expired. And, as long as the inner function exists, those outer function variables will exist in memory for the inner function to make use of. That’s a closure.

# How to make a closure

Creating a closure is as simple as returning a function from a function:

###### 

# Stepping through createGreeting

Let’s take a closer look at how `createGreeting` is working:
    
    
    **function createGreeting(greeting) {**
    **return function(name) {**
    **return `${greeting} ${name}!`;**
    **};**
    **}**
    
    **let sayHello = createGreeting("Hello");**
    **console.log(sayHello("Ben"));**

###### 

  * **First, we call createGreeting. We already know that createGreeting** **returns a function, so we create a sayHello variable and assign it that function. That function reference is linked to an environment that is the scope that existed when that function was created.**

###### 

###### 

  * **Next, we call sayHello with the argument “Ben”. To do that we evaluate the code in the function’s body, and we do that in the context of the function’s environment, like this:**

###### 

  * **Last, we return the string constructed from the name parameter and the greeting variable stored in the environment. The string is then displayed in the console.**

# Looking behind the curtain...

Let’s step through the code to see how the counter works:

  * When we call doCount (which is a reference to counter) and need to get the value of count, we use the count variable that’s in the closure’s environment. The outside world (the global scope) never sees the variable count, but we can use it anytime we call doCount. And there’s no other way to get to count except by calling doCount.

  * We call the function doCount. This executes the body of the counter function.

  * When we encounter the variable count, we notice it’s not a local variable, so we look it up in the environment and retrieve its value. We increment count, save the new value back into the environment, and return that new value to where doCount was called.

  * We repeat steps 2 and 3 each time we call doCount.

# there are no Dumb Questions

**Q:** **Do I need to understand how the environment works in detail?**

**A:** No. What you need to understand are the lexical scoping rules for JavaScript variables, and we’ve covered those. But now you know that if you have a function that is returned from within a function, it carries its environment around with it as part of a closure.

**Q:** **Do all functions come with an environment, or just ones returned from another function?**

**A:** All functions have an environment, so technically, all functions are part of a closure. It’s just that many times, that closure is empty. We’re looking at specific examples of when the closure contains varibles and their values.

**Q:** **Is there a name for the variables included in the environment?**

**A:** Yes, the variables in the environment are called “free variables” because they weren’t defined in the local scope of the function that was returned, they were defined in the enclosing scope. So, count was a free variable in counter (i.e., not locally defined), but it was needed to execute the doCount function, and that’s why it’s included in the closure.

###### 

**Closures use one shared environment, not a copy.** One thing that often misleads people learning about closures is that they think the environment in the closure must have a copy of all the variables and their values. It doesn’t. In fact, the environment references the live variables being used by your code, so if a value is changed by code outside your closure function, that new value is seen by your closure function when it is evaluated. Likewise, if there are multiple closures with the same variable in their environments, it’s all the same variable, not copies of it.

Let’s look at an example to demonstrate this.

###### 

###### 

**Yes, it is.** Whenever you create a function that executes “later” and relies on the values of variables in scope when that function was created but not in scope when the function executes, it’s a closure. We say it’s a closure because that function has an environment that contains one or more values it needs when it executes.

Let’s step through how `makeTimer` works to see what’s going on, and how the value of `doneMessage` is changed in the environment.

###### 

# How makeTimer works

  * After defining the function makeTimer, we call makeTimer with a string for doneMessage and a value of 5000 for n:

###### 

  * We call setTimeout and pass to it a function expression that will need the variable doneMessage when it runs. A closure is created containing the function along with a reference to the environment.
        
        **setTimeout(function() {**
        **alert(doneMessage);**
        **}, n);**

  * We change the value of doneMessage to “OUCH!” in code outside of the closure. That changes doneMessage in the **same environment** that is used by the closure.
        
        **doneMessage = "OUCH!";**

  * makeTimer finishes up and its scope disappears. 5,000 milliseconds (5 seconds) later, the function in the closure is called. This function references the doneMessage variable, which is now set to “OUCH!” in the environment, so we see “OUCH!” in the alert.

###### 




> **Remember that JavaScript functions are always evaluated in the environment in which they were defined.**

# Bullet Points

  * **Default parameters** are default values assigned to parameters if the corresponding arguments are missing.

  * **Rest parameters** use the rest operator, ... (three dots), to bundle up all the arguments passed to a function into one array.

  * The **spread operator** , also three dots (...) allows you to spread out an object or array into function parameters, or into another object or array.

  * Function **hoisting** refers to the first pass the browser’s JavaScript interpreter takes over your code to find all the function declarations and define them, before it does a second pass to execute your code.

  * Function expressions (including arrow functions) assigned to variables are not hoisted, so they are evaluated and executed in the second pass.

  * **Nested functions** are functions defined inside another function.

  * A nested function has local scope, just like other local variables.

  * **Lexical scope** means that we can determine the scope of a variable by reading the structure of our code.

  * **Scope** determines where your variables and functions are visible.

  * You can use scope to **encapsulate** data and computation to limit its visibility and accessibility to code outside a function.

  * Two important rules of scope are how to determine where a variable is defined by searching enclosing blocks, and that functions are evaluated in the environment in which they were defined.

  * A **closure** is a function linked with an environment.

  * A closure captures the values of variables in scope at the time the function is defined and the closure is created.

  * If you execute a function closure in a different context in which it was created, the values of **free variables** are determined by the closure’s environment.

  * Closures are usually created by returning a function from a function, or passing a function to another function (i.e., with higher-order functions).

# Chapter 12. Advanced Object Construction: **_Creating objects_**

###### 

**So far, we’ve been crafting objects by hand.** For each object, we’ve used an **object literal** to specify each and every property. That’s okay on a small scale, but for serious code we need something better. That’s where **classes** come in. With classes we can create objects much more easily, and we can create objects that all adhere to the same **design blueprint** —meaning we can use classes to ensure each object has the same properties and includes the same methods. And with classes we can write object code that is much more **concise** and a lot less error-prone when we’re creating lots of objects. So, let’s get started...

# Creating objects with object literals

So far in this book, you’ve been using _object literals_ to create objects. With an object literal, you create an object by writing it out...well, literally. Like this:
    
    
    **let taxi = {** 
    **make: "Webville Motors",**
    **model: "Taxi",**
    **year: 1955,**
    **color: "yellow",**
    **passengers: 4,**
    **convertible: false,**
    **mileage: 281341,** 
    **started: false,**
    
    **start() { this.started = true;},**
    **stop() { this.started = false;},**
    **drive() {**
    **// drive code here**
    **}**
    **};**

###### 

Object literals give you a convenient way to create objects anywhere in your code, but if you need to create lots of objects—say, a whole fleet of taxis—you wouldn’t want to have to type in a hundred different object literals, would you?

# Brain Power

###### 

Think about creating a fleet of taxi objects. What other issues might using object literals cause?

  * Tired fingers from a lot of typing!

  * Can you ensure that each taxi has the same properties? What if you make a mistake or typo, or leave out a property?

  * A lot of object literals means a lot of code. Isn’t that going to lead to slow download times for the browser?

  * The code for the start, stop, and drive methods would have to be duplicated over and over.

  * What if you decide to add or delete a property (or to change the way start or stop works)? You’d have to make the change in all the taxi objects.




# Using conventions for objects

The other thing we’ve been doing so far is creating objects _by convention_. For example, we’ve been putting properties and methods together and saying “it’s a car” or “it’s a dog,” but the only thing that makes two such objects cars or dogs is that we’ve followed our own conventions.

Again, this technique might work on a small scale, but it’s problematic when we have lots of objects, or even lots of developers working on the same code who might not fully know or follow the conventions.

But don’t take our word for it. Take a look at the following objects, which we’ve been told are all cars:

###### 

###### 

# Introducing classes

_Classes_ are your path to better object creation. Think of a class like a little blueprint for creating an endless number of similar objects.

The best way to see how classes work is to create one. Let’s revisit our old friend the dog object, and define a class to create as many dogs as we need. Here’s a version of the dog object we’ve used before, with a name, a breed, and a weight.

###### 

###### 

Now, if we were going to define such a dog with an object literal, it would look like this:
    
    
    **let dog = {**
    **name: "Fido",**
    **breed: "Mixed",**
    **weight: 38**
    **};**

###### Note

Just a simple dog object created by an object literal. Now we need to figure out how to create a lot of these puppies.

But we don’t want _just a Fido_ dog, we want a way to create _any dog_ that has a name, a breed, and a weight:

###### 

JavaScript classes are going to help us do that. Let’s see how.

# How to define a class

Creating an object from a class is a two-step process: first we define a class, and then we use it to create objects. Let’s first focus on how to define a class.

###### 

What we want is a class that we can use to create dogs, and more specifically, dogs with names, breeds, and weights. So, we’re going to define a class named Dog using the class keyword. We’re also going to add a special function to the class, called the `constructor`, that helps us create dog objects.

# How to create an object from a class

Now that we have a Dog class, let’s use it to create a new dog. To do that we use JavaScript’s `new` operator, like this:

###### 

So, to create a new dog object with a name of “Fido”, a breed of “Mixed”, and a weight of 38, we start with the `new` keyword and follow it with the class name, and then with the arguments we want to pass to the constructor function. After this statement is evaluated, the variable `fido` will hold a reference to our new dog object.

Now that we have a class for dogs, we can keep making them:
    
    
    **let fluffy = new Dog("Fluffy", "Poodle", 30);**
    **let spot = new Dog("Spot", "Chihuahua", 10);**

That’s a bit easier than using object literals, isn’t it? And by creating dog objects this way, we can ensure that each dog has the same set of properties: `name`, `breed`, and `weight`.

# How classes work

We’ve seen how to declare a class and how to use it to create objects, but we should also take a look behind the scenes to see how a class actually works. Here’s the key: to understand classes we need to know what the `new` operator is doing.

###### 

We’ll begin with the statement we used to create `fido`:
    
    
    **let fido = new Dog("Fido", "Mixed", 38);**

Take a look at the righthand side of the assignment, where all the action is. Let’s follow its execution:

  * The first thing `new` does is create a new, empty object.

###### 

  * Next, `new` sets `this` to point to the new object.

###### 

  * With `this` set up, the constructor is called and passed “Fido”, “Mixed”, and 38 as arguments.

###### 

  * Finally, once the Dog class constructor function has completed, the `new` operator returns `this`, which is a reference to the newly created object. Notice `this` is returned for you; you don’t have to explicitly return it in your code. And after the new object has been returned, we assign it to the variable `fido`.

######

# there are no Dumb Questions

**Q:** **Why do class names start with a capital letter?**

**A:** This is a convention that JavaScript developers use so they can easily identify classes from their names in code (say, compared to variable names and function names).

**Q:** **Other than setting up the properties of the** `this` **object, is a class’s constructor function just like a regular object method?**

**A:** If you mean computationally, yes. You can do anything in a constructor you can do in a regular function, like declare and use variables, use for loops, call other functions, and so on. The only thing you don’t want to do is return a value (other than this) from a class’s constructor, because that will cause the constructor to not return the object it’s supposed to be constructing.

**Q:** **Do the parameter names of a class’s constructor function have to match the property names?**

**A:** No. You can use whatever names you want for the parameters. The parameters are just used to hold values that we want to assign to the object’s properties to customize the object. What matters are the names of the properties you use for the object. That said, we often do use the same names for clarity, so we know which properties we’re assigning by looking at the constructor function definition in a class.

**Q:** **Is it better to create objects with a class than with object literals?**

**A:** Both are useful. A class is useful when you want to create lots of objects with the same property names and methods. Using them is convenient, promotes code reuse, and provides consistency across your objects.

But sometimes we just need a quick object, perhaps a one-time-use-only object, and literals are a good option here; they are concise and expressive.

So it really depends what your needs are. Both are great ways to create an object.

**Q:** **I’m still a bit confused by** `this` **in a class’s constructor function. We’re using** `this` **to assign properties to the object, and we’re also using** `this` **in the methods of the object. Are these the same thing?**

**A:** When you use a class to create an object the value of **this** is set to the new object, so all the code that is evaluated in the constructor applies to that new object.

Later, when you call a method on an object, **this** is set to the object whose method you called. So, the **this** in your methods will always refer to the object whose method was called.

**Q:** **Is an object created by a class just like an object created with a literal?**

**A:** Mostly. We’ll get into some differences shortly.

> **A class is useful when you want to create lots of objects with the same property names and methods. Using classes is convenient, promotes code reuse, and provides consistency across your objects.**

# The Class Exposed

###### 

**This week’s interview:**

**Getting to know class**

**Head First:** `class`, where have you been hiding? How did we get to Chapter 12 before seeing you?

**class:** There are still a lot of scripts out there that don’t use me, or use me without understanding me.

**Head First:** Why is that?

**class:** Because many scripters just use object literals or copy and paste code that uses me, without understanding how I work.

**Head First:** That’s a good point...object literals are convenient, and I myself am not quite clear on when or how to use you just yet.

**class:** Well it’s true, I am kind of an advanced feature. After all, to know how to use me, you first have to know how objects work, and how functions work, and how `this` works...it’s a lot to wrap your head around before you even learn about me at all!

**Head First:** Can you give us the elevator pitch about yourself? Now that our readers know about objects, functions, and `this`, it would be great for them to get motivated for learning about you.

**class:** Let me think for a second...Okay, here you go: I work with the `new` operator to create new objects.

**Head First:** Umm, I hate to break it to you, but that isn’t the best elevator pitch.

**class:** Gimme a break...I’m a language feature; I’m not in PR.

**Head First:** Well, you do raise several questions with that pitch. First of all, why do you need an operator?

**class:** It’s just the way I work. `new` and I work together. `new` says “create a new, empty object, and then call my constructor function.” Then my constructor function does all the work of setting up the properties for the new object. We make a good team.

**Head First:** Wait, that was too fast. One step at a time, please.

**class:** Well, first, `new` makes a new object. Everyone thinks that my constructor function is what does it, but it’s actually `new`. I often say `new` is seriously underrated.

**Head First:** Go on...

**class:** Okay, so `new` then calls my constructor function and makes sure that the new object is referenced by the `this` keyword in the body of my function.

**Head First:** Why?

**class:** So that the statements in the body of my constructor function have a way to refer to the object. After all, the whole point of a constructor function is to extend that object using new properties and methods. If you’re using the constructor to create objects like dogs and cars, you’re going to want those objects to have some properties, right?

**Head First:** Right. And then?

**class:** Then I make sure that the new object that was created is returned from the constructor. It’s a nice convenience so that developers don’t have to remember to return it themselves.

**Head First:** It sounds very convenient. Now why would anyone use an object literal after learning about you?

**class:** Oh, object literal and I go way back. He’s a great guy, and I’d use him in a second if I had to create a quick object. But you want me when you’ve got to create a lot of similar objects, when you want to make sure your objects are taking advantage of code reuse, when you want to ensure some consistency, and, after you’ve learned a little more, to support some even more advanced uses.

**Head First:** More advanced? Oh, do tell!

**class:** Now now, let’s keep these readers focused. They’ve barely even started learning about me!

## Overriding Car’s drive method

Next, let’s add the methods. We want the RocketCar to drive sort of like a Car, except the RocketCar has thrusters, and if the thrusters are on, then the RocketCar will drive much faster than a regular Car. So, we need to give the RocketCar its own `drive` method.

We know that the RocketCar class inherits the `drive` method from Car, but that drive method isn’t quite right. We’ll implement our own `drive` method in RocketCar that _overrides_ the Car class’s `drive` method, so if we call the `drive` method on a RocketCar object we’ll get the behavior defined in RocketCar’s `drive` method, not the behavior defined in Car’s `drive` method:

###### 

###### 

**Mara** : Maybe. I’m wondering if we can reuse the code in the Car class’s `drive` method using `super`.

**Alex** : You mean like we do in the constructor? I thought that was a special constructor thing.

**Mara** : No, I think we can use it whenever we want to refer to a method in a class’s superclass.

**Sam** : Superclass?

**Mara** : Right, the class that another class is extending with the `extends` keyword is called a superclass. That’s why we refer to it using `super`.

**Alex** : Oh...interesting.

**Mara** : We should be able to write:
    
    
    **super.drive();**

to refer to Car’s drive method.

**Sam** : But we didn’t write:
    
    
    **super.constructor();**

when we wanted to call Car’s constructor, right? We just wrote:
    
    
    **super(...);**

**Mara** : Right. The constructor is a special case. If you say `super``()` you’re referring to the constructor, but if you say `super.drive()` you’re referring to a specific method.

**Sam** : Why can’t we just write `this.drive()`?

**Mara** : Because that would refer to the `drive` method in the RocketCar class, and we want to refer to the `drive` method in the Car class.

**Sam** : Ohhh...

**Mara** : Let’s give `super.drive``()` a try and see if it works.

# Finishing up the RocketCar class

Okay, it’s time to finish the RocketCar class. We have the constructor in place, and we have a general idea of what we want the `drive` method to do—behave like Car’s `drive` method but with extra thrust—so let’s write the code. We’re going to add the `drive` method along with a `thrust` method to turn on and off the thruster:

###### 

## What’s happening here?

Like Taxi, RocketCar extends Car, so it _inherits_ the Car class’s properties and methods. RocketCar _extends_ Car by adding new properties and methods (`thrustOn` and `thrust`), and _overrides_ the `drive` method from Car so it can drive differently than a Car. But we reuse Car’s `drive` code by calling `super.drive()` in RocketCar’s `drive` method, before adding special `drive` behavior for the RocketCar.

######

# there are no Dumb Questions

**Q:** **Can you explain the difference between extends and inherits again? I’m not quite sure I’ve got it right.**

**A:** We use the extends keyword to specify that a class inherits its properties and methods from another class, and potentially adds new properties and methods. For instance, the Taxi class extends the Car class, and, more informally, it inherits its properties and methods from the Car class. The Taxi class also adds new properties and methods to those it inherited from the Car class.

**Q:** **So the class that’s being extended is the superclass, right? What’s the class that’s doing the extending called?**

**A:** We call the class that extends a superclass the subclass. So Car is the superclass of Taxi and RocketCar, and Taxi and RocketCar are subclasses of Car.

**Q:** **So if you define a method in a superclass and define a method with the same name in the subclass, the method in the subclass always overrides the method in the superclass?**

**A:** That’s right. So, if you create a Car object, like a fiat, using the Car class, if you call fiat.drive() you’ll get the behavior of Car’s drive method. If you create a RocketCar object, because RocketCar extends Car and overrides drive, if you call rocketCar.drive() you’ll get the behavior defined in the RocketCar class.

**Q:** **Can I override properties and use super with a property name to get access to properties in the superclass?**

**A:** No, this only works with methods. You can override properties in a subclass, but you can’t use super.propertyName to access a property defined in the superclass.

**Q:** **When you override a method from a superclass, do you always need to call super.[that method]?**

**A:** No. You only need to use super to call the superclass’s method if you want to reuse that method’s behavior, like we did in RocketCar. Otherwise, you can define entirely new behavior for your subclass.

###### 

**Would you believe, object literals?** We’ve had some discussion of classes versus object literals, and mentioned that object literals are still quite useful, but you really haven’t seen a good example of that. Well, the Car class’s constructor function is a perfect example of where by using an object literal we can clean up our code a bit and make it more readable and maintainable.

Let’s take a look at that now...

# Using an object literal to clean up our constructor

Let’s look at the Car class again and see how we might be able to clean it up a bit:

###### 

# Creating the object literal for the cadi parameters

So, the problem we’re highlighting here is that we have a heck of a lot of parameters in the Car class’s constructor function, making it difficult to read and maintain. While that might seem like a minor inconvenience, it actually causes more bugs than you might think—and not only that, but they’re often nasty bugs that are hard to diagnose at first.

###### Note

They’re hard to diagnose because if you switch two variables, the code is still syntactically correct, but it doesn’t function correctly because you’ve switched two values.

###### Note

Or, if you leave out a value, all kinds of craziness can ensue!

However, there is a common technique that we can use when passing all these arguments that can be used for any function, whether or not it’s a constructor function in a class. The technique works like this: take all your arguments, throw them in an object literal, and then pass that literal to your function—that way, you’re passing all your values in one container (the literal object) and you don’t have worry about matching the order of your arguments and parameters.

Let’s rewrite the code to call Car’s constructor function, and then do a slight rework of the constructor code to see how this works. Let’s start by taking the call to Car’s constructor function and reworking its arguments into an object literal:

###### 

And then we can rewrite the call to the Car constructor like this:

######

# Accessor properties

Webville Motors wants to expand its business to the European market, so we need a way to get the mileage of a car in kilometers (kms) rather than miles. What’s the best way to do that?

###### 

We could use two separate properties, but then we’d have to keep track of both miles and kilometers. Or, we could write a method, `getKm`, that takes the current mileage of the car, converts the mileage to kilometers, and returns the kilometers...but then we’d have to remember to access the miles via the property and the kms via the method. Hmmm...

There is a better way. So far, all the properties in our objects have been _data properties_. Data properties are all specified using a property name and value, like we’ve done so far in object literals, and like we are doing in the Car class above.

JavaScript objects have another kind of property, an _accessor property_. An accessor property is defined using a special method, but the property is accessed as if it were a regular (data) property. To create a _getter_ method for an accessor property (that is, a way to _get_ the value of the property), we use the keyword **get** in front of a method that returns a value for the property, like this:

###### 

Now that we’ve defined a getter method, we can use it to get the value of the `km` accessor property. Turn the page to see how...

# Using getters

We have a getter method, `km`, in our Car class now, so let’s use it to get the kilometers that a Car object has been driven. To use an accessor property, we write that property exactly as if it were a regular (data) property, but behind the scenes the getter method is called. So, to get the value of the `km` accessor property of a `fiat`, we’d write:
    
    
    **fiat.km**

###### Note

No () needed!

We do not use parentheses to get the value of an accessor property, even though that property is defined using a method. Because of that special keyword `**get**`, we can treat `km` like a regular property.

Here’s what this looks like in our code. We’ll create a `fiat` object from the Car class with the `km` accessor property and getter method, and then use the `km` property:

###### 

# Test Drive

###### 

Update the fiat, and test your code. Make sure your result matches ours.

###### 

# What’s a getter without a setter?

**With only a getter method, an accessor property is read-only:**

With just a getter method, the `km` property is read-only. You can’t set the value of the `km` property to a new value. If you try to assign a new value to the property, like this, nothing will change:
    
    
    **fiat.km = 141641;**

###### Note

And note, you will not see an error if you try this!

Of course, the fiat’s `mileage` property is a regular data property, so we can change that no problem:
    
    
    **fiat.mileage = 88020;**

###### Note

This is 141,641 kms.

And then the next time we get the kilometers, we’ll get the updated value since the value of the `km` property is always computed by the getter method dynamically:

So, we could update the fiat using the mileage property each time, but that’s not as convenient for the European driver.

###### 

###### 

**To set the value of an accessor property, we need a setter method:**

To update the mileage of the fiat using kilometers, we can create a setter method for the `km` accessor property. We use the keyword **set** , and create a method with one parameter, the new value for the property:
    
    
    **set km(newKm) {** 
    **this.mileage = newKm / 1.60934;** 
    **}**

We don’t store the kilometers value directly; we convert it to miles and update the mileage property. The mileage property always holds the current number of miles on the car. To set the new mileage of the car using `km`, we write:
    
    
    **fiat.km = 141641;**

###### Note

This is 88020 miles.

###### 

This time it works!

# Test Drive

###### 

Add the setter method to the `fiat`, and test your Car class and `fiat`. The complete code is below.

###### 

###### 

**Yes, you can.** And it’s easy. All you have to do is add the keyword `**get**` in front of an object’s method to get an accessor property and the keyword `**set**` in front of an object’s method to set the value of an accessor property, just like you do in a class. Let’s dig out our old code for `fiat` (the code that uses an object literal to create the `fiat` object) and add the `km` accessor property:

**Sam** : You mean a count of all the cars that have been instantiated using the `new` operator?

**Mara** : Exactly.

**Alex** : That makes sense, but can’t we just have a global counter that we always increment when a car is created?

**Mara** : Well, what if someone forgets to increment it? Or someone even decrements it? From a code perspective, that doesn’t sound very safe.

**Sam** : I’m trying to think of how we could do that. When I create a new object, all the properties in that object belong, well, to the object. A counter is pointless if we make it part of the the car objects we create from Car. Every counter will always be 1!

**Mara** : Exactly my thinking. So what if we make it part of the class, not the object?

**Alex** : Wait, what is this magic you speak of? Part of the class??

**Mara** : It is a bit like magic, Alex. It’s called a **static** property. We can make static methods too, if we want. Because there is only one class, there will be only one `counter` property.

**Sam** : Sounds very promising.

**Mara** : I think so, and I think this will be straightforward. Let’s give it a try and see if we can quickly get this new feature out to folks.

# Static properties and methods

Static properties and methods are similar to object properties and methods, except they belong to a class. For properties, that means there is only one property no matter how many objects you instantiate from a class, because there is only one class. For methods, that means we can call a method on a class without having to have an object instantiated. To add a static property or method, we use the `**static**` keyword:

###### 

###### 

In the Widget class, we have a static property, `counter`, and a static method, `getCounter`. The `counter` property is a property of the class, and the `getCounter` method is a method of the class. Neither of these are added to objects that we instantiate from Widget.

######

# Counting our car production

We can use the exact same idea as we just used for counting widgets to count cars. We’ll just add a static `counter` property to the Car class, along with a static `getCounter` method to get the current counter value. Looks like someone is thinking ahead and has added a static `resetCounter` method in case we ever want to reset the counter:

###### 

###### 

**Sam** : I got this! Check out my code. I added the static `counter` property and a static method to get the current value of the counter. I even threw in a `resetCounter` method in case we want to do a “factory reset.”

**Mara** : Good thinking Sam, and great job.

**Alex** : Wait, don’t we need to add the counter to Taxi and RocketCar too?

**Mara** : No...remember, Taxi and RocketCar extend the Car class. In our Taxi and RocketCar constructors, we’re calling `super(...)`, which calls the Car constructor, and that’s where the counter is incremented.

**Alex** : Ohhhh, that makes sense. So if we forgot to call `super``(...)` in the Taxi and RocketCar constructors, we’d have a problem, right?

**Mara** : Right. But we’re calling `super(...)`, and typically that’s what we do when we extend a class, so we’ll have no problem keeping the counter accurate for Cars, Taxis, and RocketCars.

**Sam** : See, told ya. I got this!

    **};**
    **let rocketCar = new RocketCar(rocketCarParams);**

    **// use only if needed to do a "factory reset"!**
    **Car.resetCounter();**
    **console.log("How many cars have we made?", Car.getCounter());**
    
    **// remake the rocketCar**
    **rocketCar = new RocketCar(rocketCarParams);**
    **console.log("How many cars have we made?", Car.getCounter());**

# Bullet Points

  * An **object literal** works well when you need to create a small number of objects.

  * A **class** works well when you need to create many similar objects.

  * We capitalize the first letter of class names by convention.

  * Using a class, we can create objects that are consistent, having the same property names and methods.

  * Use the **new** operator with a class to call the class’s constructor function and create an object.

  * When you use new with a class, it creates a new, empty object, which is assigned to **this** within the body of the constructor function.

  * Use **this** in a class’s constructor function to access the object being constructed and add properties to the object.

  * A new object is returned automatically by the class’s constructor function.

  * If you forget to use **new** with a class, no object is created. This will cause errors in your code that can be difficult to debug.

  * To customize objects, we pass arguments to the class’s constructor function and use those values to initialize the properties of the object being created.

  * If a class’s constructor function has a lot of parameters, consider consolidating them into one object parameter.

  * Using the **extends** keyword, we can extend one class with another.

  * When you extend a class, the class being extended is called the **superclass** , and the class doing the extending is called the **subclass**.

  * When we extend a class, the subclass **inherits** properties and methods form the superclass.

  * The **super** keyword allows us to call the constructor and other methods in the superclass.

  * You may **override** methods and properties from the superclass in the subclass.

  * Use the **set** and **get** keywords to create **accessor properties** : setter and getter methods that are treated like properties.

  * **Static** properties belong to a class. If you define a static property in a class, there is only one of them, no matter how many objects you make from that class.

  * Static methods also belong to a class. To invoke them you need to invoke them on the class, not an instance of the class.

  * [Chapter 1](ch01.html#a_quick_dip_into_javascript_getting_you)

**JavaScript gives you superpowers**. The **true programming language** of the web, JavaScript lets you **add behavior** to your web pages. No more dry, boring, static pages that just sit there looking at you—with JavaScript, you’ll be able to reach out and touch your users, react to interesting events, grab data from the web to use in your pages, draw graphics right into those pages, and a lot more. And once you know JavaScript, you’ll also be in a position to create **totally new** behaviors for your users.

You’ll be in good company, too. JavaScript’s not only one of the **most popular** programming languages, it’s also **supported** in all modern browsers and is used in many environments outside of the browser. More on that later; for now, let’s get started!

**Get ready for your first superpower.** You’ve got some programming under your belt; now it’s time to really move things along with **functions**. Functions give you the power to write code that can be applied to all sorts of different circumstances, code that can be **reused** over and over, code that is much more **manageable,** code that can be **abstracted** away and given a simple name so you can forget all the complexity and get on with the important stuff. You’re going to find not only that functions are your gateway from scripter to programmer, but that they’re the key to the JavaScript programming style. In this chapter, we’re going to start with the basics—the mechanics, the ins and outs of how functions really work—and then you’ll keep honing your function skills throughout the rest of the book. So, let’s get a good foundation started, _now_.

  * [Chapter 4](ch04.html#putting_some_order_in_your_data_arrys)

**There’s more to JavaScript than numbers, strings, and booleans.** So far you’ve been writing JavaScript code with **primitives** —strings, numbers, and booleans, like “Fido”, 23, and true. You can do a lot with primitive types, but at some point you’ve got to deal with **more data**. Say, all the items in a shopping cart, or all the songs in a playlist, or a set of stars and their apparent magnitude, or an entire product catalog. For that you need a little more _oomph_. The type of choice for this kind of ordered data is a JavaScript **array** , and in this chapter we’re going to walk through how to put your data into an array, how to pass it around, and how to operate on it. We’ll be looking at a few other ways to **structure your data** in later chapters, but let’s get started with arrays.

**It’s time to get serious about our types.** One of the great things about JavaScript is you can get a long way without knowing a lot of details of the language.
